          <div class="SongPageGriddesktop__OneColumn-sc-1px5b71-0 fthhzM About__Grid-ut4i9m-0 endauX">
            <div class="About__Container-ut4i9m-1 iDZxCx">
              <h1 font-size="xxLargeHeadline" class="About__Title-ut4i9m-2 kcXwIY">About</h1>
              <div class="Attribution__Container-sc-1nmry9o-0 dhIhSa">
                <div class="Attribution__Header-sc-1nmry9o-1 iPNsXE"><span>Genius Annotation</span><button text-decoration="underline" type="button" class="TextButton-sc-192nsqv-0 ixMmYX">3 contributors</button></div>
              </div>
              <div class="ExpandableContent__Container-sc-1165iv-0 ikywhQ">
                <div class="ExpandableContent__Content-sc-1165iv-2 jshzGo">
                  <div class="SongDescription__Content-sc-615rvk-1 HtOlE">
                    <div class="RichText__Container-oz284w-0 lksWXY">
                      <p>Rick Rubin, who produced <em>Toxicity</em> for System of a Down, cites this song as one of his most important projects. He told <a href="http://www.rollingstone.com/music/lists/rick-rubin-my-life-in-21-songs-20160211/system-of-a-down-chop-suey-2001-20160209#ixzz4B7yBs8xh" rel="noopener nofollow"><em>Rolling Stone</em></a>:</p>

                      <blockquote>
                        <p>This song was originally going to be called “Self-Righteous Suicide,” and the record company rebelled. I remember wanting to go to the mat and keep the title, and the band decided, “Let’s call it ‘Chop Suey!’” which I thought was kind of funny.</p>
                      </blockquote>

                      <p>Chop-suey is the Americanization of the Chinese quick dish <em>tsap sui,</em> which means “odds and ends, mixed bits” in the Cantonese dialect. The song title is a play on “Self-right-Chop Suey-cide,” that has a further sort of humor in that they mixed up a controversial word into a nonsensical word to create a more ‘radio-friendly’ title.</p>

                      <p>The song enjoyed success, climbing the charts after its August 2001 release before virtually disappearing from airwaves after 9/11. The song’s chorus, with its congratulatory “self-righteous suicide,” and lament, “I cry when angels deserve to die” was considered a bit too raw for listeners at the time. In the wake of the tragedy, Clear Channel Communications (now iHeartMedia) put out <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2001_Clear_Channel_memorandum" rel="noopener nofollow">a memo</a> to its subsidiary radio stations temporarily blackballing (but not outright banning) it, along with 164 other “lyrically questionable” songs. Eventually, the record resurfaced on the charts—in part due to its earlier popularity, in part due to the lack of new singles released that September. However, it remains one of System of a Down’s worst performing US singles, particularly relative to the song’s global performance.</p>

                      <p>Although the lyrics are viewed as confrontational, the singer is actually challenging the listener to “wake up” and reconsider the way that they judge and deride people who are engaged in self-destructive behaviors. The song culminates in a biblical connection to Jesus before his death on the cross, with the singer ostensibly having chosen his “self-righteous suicide” so that these ‘sinners’ would be forgiven.</p>
                    </div>
                  </div>
                </div>
                <div class="ExpandableContent__ButtonContainer-sc-1165iv-1 fIaatV"><button class="Button-sc-1874dbw-0 jhtDWk">Expand

                        <div class="RichText__Container-oz284w-0 lksWXY">
                          <blockquote>
                            <p>The song is about how when people die, they will be regarded differently depending on the way they pass. Like, if I were to die from a drug overdose, everyone would say I deserved it because I abused drugs, hence the line ‘Angels deserve to die.’</p>
                          </blockquote>

                          <p>—<a href="http://www.songfacts.com/detail.php?id=2425" rel="noopener nofollow">Daron Malakian</a></p>

                          <blockquote>
                            <p>I wrote that in the back of the RV. With that [song], I didn’t want to repeat myself. I didn’t want to write <a href="https://genius.com/System-of-a-down-pluck-politically-lying-unholy-cowardly-killers-lyrics" rel="noopener" data-api_path="/songs/151749">“P.L.U.C.K.”</a> again or <a href="https://genius.com/System-of-a-down-suite-pee-lyrics" rel="noopener" data-api_path="/songs/62874">“Suite-Pee”</a> again. I wanted to explore something different. At the same time, I wanted to retain our style so I put a lot of the ideas together.</p>
                          </blockquote>

                          <p>—<a href="https://www.revolvermag.com/music/system-downs-toxicity-10-things-you-didnt-know" rel="noopener nofollow">Daron Malakian, <em>Louder Than Hell</em></a></p>

                      <div class="Question__Body-sc-2pyi4c-2 fZoegP">
                        <div class="RichText__Container-oz284w-0 lksWXY">
                          <p>“Chop Suey!” was <a href="https://www.riaa.com/gold-platinum/?tab_active=default-award&amp;ar=system+of+a+down&amp;ti=chop+suey%21&amp;lab=american&amp;genre=&amp;format=Single&amp;date_option=release&amp;from=&amp;to=&amp;award=&amp;type=&amp;category=&amp;adv=SEARCH#search_section" rel="noopener nofollow">certified gold</a> by the RIAA on March 6, 2006.</p>

                      <div class="Question__Footer-sc-2pyi4c-9 iJaJBc">

data-song-id=\'88430\'>Read <a href=\'https://genius.com/System-of-a-down-chop-suey-lyrics\'>“Chop Suey!” by System of a Down<\/a> on Genius<\/div> <script crossorigin src=\'//genius.com/songs/88430/embed.js\'><\/script>\",\"descriptionPreview\":\"Rick Rubin, who produced Toxicity for System of a Down, cites this song as one of his most important projects. He told Rolling Stone:\\n\\nThis song was originally going to be called “Self-Righteous Suicide,” and the record company rebelled. I remember wanting to go to the mat and keep the title, and the band decided, “Let’s call it ‘Chop Suey!’” which I thought was kind of funny.\\n\\nChop-suey is the Americanization of the Chinese quick dish tsap sui, which means “odds and ends, mixed bits” in the Cantonese dialect. The song title is a play on “Self-right-Chop Suey-cide,” that has a further sort of humor in that they mixed up a controversial word into a nonsensical word to create a more ‘radio-friendly’ title.\\n\\nThe song enjoyed success, climbing the charts after its August 2001 release before virtually disappearing from airwaves after 9/11. The song’s chorus, with its congratulatory “self-righteous suicide,” and lament, “I cry when angels deserve to die” was considered a bit too raw for listeners at the time. In the wake of the tragedy, Clear Channel Communications (now iHeartMedia) put out a memo to its subsidiary radio stations  temporarily blackballing (but not outright banning) it, along with 164 other “lyrically questionable” songs. Eventually, the record resurfaced on the charts—in part due to its earlier popularity, in part due to the lack of new singles released that September. However, it remains one of System of a Down’s worst performing US singles, particularly relative to the song’s global performance.\\n\\nAlthough the lyrics are viewed as confrontational, the singer is actually challenging the listener to “wake up” and reconsider the way that they judge and deride people who are engaged in self-destructive behaviors. The song culminates in a biblical connection to Jesus before his death on the cross, with the singer ostensibly having chosen his “self-righteous suicide” so that these ‘sinners’ would be forgiven.\",\"description\":{\"markdown\":\"Rick Rubin, who produced *Toxicity* for System of a Down, cites this song as one of his most important projects. He told [*Rolling Stone*](http://www.rollingstone.com/music/lists/rick-rubin-my-life-in-21-songs-20160211/system-of-a-down-chop-suey-2001-20160209#ixzz4B7yBs8xh):\\n\\n>This song was originally going to be called \\\"Self-Righteous Suicide,\\\" and the record company rebelled. I remember wanting to go to the mat and keep the title, and the band decided, \\\"Let\'s call it \'Chop Suey!\'\\\" which I thought was kind of funny.\\n\\nChop-suey is the Americanization of the Chinese quick dish *tsap sui,* which means \\\"odds and ends, mixed bits\\\" in the Cantonese dialect. The song title is a play on \\\"Self-right-Chop Suey-cide,\\\" that has a further sort of humor in that they mixed up a controversial word into a nonsensical word to create a more \'radio-friendly\' title.\\n\\nThe song enjoyed success, climbing the charts after its August 2001 release before virtually disappearing from airwaves after 9/11. The song\'s chorus, with its congratulatory \\\"self-righteous suicide,\\\" and lament, \\\"I cry when angels deserve to die\\\" was considered a bit too raw for listeners at the time. In the wake of the tragedy, Clear Channel Communications (now iHeartMedia) put out [a memo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2001_Clear_Channel_memorandum) to its subsidiary radio stations  temporarily blackballing (but not outright banning) it, along with 164 other \\\"lyrically questionable\\\" songs. Eventually, the record resurfaced on the charts---in part due to its earlier popularity, in part due to the lack of new singles released that September. However, it remains one of System of a Down\'s worst performing US singles, particularly relative to the song\'s global performance.\\n\\nAlthough the lyrics are viewed as confrontational, the singer is actually challenging the listener to \\\"wake up\\\" and reconsider the way that they judge and deride people who are engaged in self-destructive behaviors. The song culminates in a biblical connection to Jesus before his death on the cross, with the singer ostensibly having chosen his \\\"self-righteous suicide\\\" so that these \'sinners\' would be forgiven.\",\"html\":\"<p>Rick Rubin, who produced <em>Toxicity<\/em> for System of a Down, cites this song as one of his most important projects. He told <a href=\\\"http://www.rollingstone.com/music/lists/rick-rubin-my-life-in-21-songs-20160211/system-of-a-down-chop-suey-2001-20160209#ixzz4B7yBs8xh\\\" rel=\\\"noopener nofollow\\\"><em>Rolling Stone<\/em><\/a>:<\/p>\\n\\n<blockquote><p>This song was originally going to be called “Self-Righteous Suicide,” and the record company rebelled. I remember wanting to go to the mat and keep the title, and the band decided, “Let’s call it ‘Chop Suey!’” which I thought was kind of funny.<\/p><\/blockquote>\\n\\n<p>Chop-suey is the Americanization of the Chinese quick dish <em>tsap sui,<\/em> which means “odds and ends, mixed bits” in the Cantonese dialect. The song title is a play on “Self-right-Chop Suey-cide,” that has a further sort of humor in that they mixed up a controversial word into a nonsensical word to create a more ‘radio-friendly’ title.<\/p>\\n\\n<p>The song enjoyed success, climbing the charts after its August 2001 release before virtually disappearing from airwaves after 9/11. The song’s chorus, with its congratulatory “self-righteous suicide,” and lament, “I cry when angels deserve to die” was considered a bit too raw for listeners at the time. In the wake of the tragedy, Clear Channel Communications (now iHeartMedia) put out <a href=\\\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2001_Clear_Channel_memorandum\\\" rel=\\\"noopener nofollow\\\">a memo<\/a> to its subsidiary radio stations  temporarily blackballing (but not outright banning) it, along with 164 other “lyrically questionable” songs. Eventually, the record resurfaced on the charts—in part due to its earlier popularity, in part due to the lack of new singles released that September. However, it remains one of System of a Down’s worst performing US singles, particularly relative to the song’s global performance.<\/p>\\n\\n<p>Although the lyrics are viewed as confrontational, the singer is actually challenging the listener to “wake up” and reconsider the way that they judge and deride people who are engaged in self-destructive behaviors. The song culminates in a biblical connection to Jesus before his death on the cross, with the singer ostensibly having chosen his “self-righteous suicide” so that these ‘sinners’ would be class=\'rg_standalone_container\' data-src=\'//genius.com/annotations/3726291/standalone_embed\'><a href=\'https://genius.com/3726291/System-of-a-down-chop-suey/Chop-suey\'>Chop Suey!<\/a><br><a src=\'//genius.com/annotations/load_standalone_embeds.js\'><\/script>\",\"deleted\":false,\"customPreview\":null,\"createdAt\":1373771096,\"community\":true,\"commentCount\":0,\"body\":{\"markdown\":\"Rick Rubin, who produced *Toxicity* for System of a Down, cites this song as one of his most important projects. He told [*Rolling Stone*](http://www.rollingstone.com/music/lists/rick-rubin-my-life-in-21-songs-20160211/system-of-a-down-chop-suey-2001-20160209#ixzz4B7yBs8xh):\\n\\n>This song was originally going to be called \\\"Self-Righteous Suicide,\\\" and the record company rebelled. I remember wanting to go to the mat and keep the title, and the band decided, \\\"Let\'s call it \'Chop Suey!\'\\\" which I thought was kind of funny.\\n\\nChop-suey is the Americanization of the Chinese quick dish *tsap sui,* which means \\\"odds and ends, mixed bits\\\" in the Cantonese dialect. The song title is a play on \\\"Self-right-Chop Suey-cide,\\\" that has a further sort of humor in that they mixed up a controversial word into a nonsensical word to create a more \'radio-friendly\' title.\\n\\nThe song enjoyed success, climbing the charts after its August 2001 release before virtually disappearing from airwaves after 9/11. The song\'s chorus, with its congratulatory \\\"self-righteous suicide,\\\" and lament, \\\"I cry when angels deserve to die\\\" was considered a bit too raw for listeners at the time. In the wake of the tragedy, Clear Channel Communications (now iHeartMedia) put out [a memo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2001_Clear_Channel_memorandum) to its subsidiary radio stations  temporarily blackballing (but not outright banning) it, along with 164 other \\\"lyrically questionable\\\" songs. Eventually, the record resurfaced on the charts---in part due to its earlier popularity, in part due to the lack of new singles released that September. However, it remains one of System of a Down\'s worst performing US singles, particularly relative to the song\'s global performance.\\n\\nAlthough the lyrics are viewed as confrontational, the singer is actually challenging the listener to \\\"wake up\\\" and reconsider the way that they judge and deride people who are engaged in self-destructive behaviors. The song culminates in a biblical connection to Jesus before his death on the cross, with the singer ostensibly having chosen his \\\"self-righteous suicide\\\" so that these \'sinners\' would be forgiven.\",\"html\":\"<p>Rick Rubin, who produced <em>Toxicity<\/em> for System of a Down, cites this song as one of his most important projects. He told <a href=\\\"http://www.rollingstone.com/music/lists/rick-rubin-my-life-in-21-songs-20160211/system-of-a-down-chop-suey-2001-20160209#ixzz4B7yBs8xh\\\" rel=\\\"noopener nofollow\\\"><em>Rolling Stone<\/em><\/a>:<\/p>\\n\\n<blockquote><p>This song was originally going to be called “Self-Righteous Suicide,” and the record company rebelled. I remember wanting to go to the mat and keep the title, and the band decided, “Let’s call it ‘Chop Suey!’” which I thought was kind of funny.<\/p><\/blockquote>\\n\\n<p>Chop-suey is the Americanization of the Chinese quick dish <em>tsap sui,<\/em> which means “odds and ends, mixed bits” in the Cantonese dialect. The song title is a play on “Self-right-Chop Suey-cide,” that has a further sort of humor in that they mixed up a controversial word into a nonsensical word to create a more ‘radio-friendly’ title.<\/p>\\n\\n<p>The song enjoyed success, climbing the charts after its August 2001 release before virtually disappearing from airwaves after 9/11. The song’s chorus, with its congratulatory “self-righteous suicide,” and lament, “I cry when angels deserve to die” was considered a bit too raw for listeners at the time. In the wake of the tragedy, Clear Channel Communications (now iHeartMedia) put out <a href=\\\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2001_Clear_Channel_memorandum\\\" rel=\\\"noopener nofollow\\\">a memo<\/a> to its subsidiary radio stations  temporarily blackballing (but not outright banning) it, along with 164 other “lyrically questionable” songs. Eventually, the record resurfaced on the charts—in part due to its earlier popularity, in part due to the lack of new singles released that September. However, it remains one of System of a Down’s worst performing US singles, particularly relative to the song’s global performance.<\/p>\\n\\n<p>Although the lyrics are viewed as confrontational, the singer is actually challenging the listener to “wake up” and reconsider the way that they judge and deride people who are engaged in self-destructive behaviors. The song culminates in a biblical connection to Jesus before his death on the cross, with the singer ostensibly having chosen his “self-righteous suicide” so that these ‘sinners’ would be forgiven.<\/p>\"},\"beingCreated\":false,\"apiPath\":\"/annotations/3726291\",\"type\":\"annotation\"}},\"referents\":{\"3726291\":{\"annotations\":[3726291],\"annotatable\":{\"url\":\"https://genius.com/System-of-a-down-chop-suey-lyrics\",\"type\":\"song\",\"title\":\"Chop Suey!\",\"linkTitle\":\"Chop Suey!
